</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. Bodies move over time in such a way that their trajectories are perpendicular to the surfaces of constant <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="S" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4611d85173cd3b508e67077d4a1252c9c05abca2" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.499ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>, analogously to how a light ray propagates in the direction perpendicular to its wavefront. This is simplest to express for the case of a single point mass, in which <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle S}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relation_to_other_physical_theories">Relation to other physical theories</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Thermodynamics_and_statistical_physics">Thermodynamics and statistical physics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Brownian_motion_large.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Brownian_motion_large.gif/220px-Brownian_motion_large.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c2/Brownian_motion_large.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Brownian_motion_large.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A simulation of a larger, but still microscopic, particle (in yellow) surrounded by a gas of smaller particles, illustrating <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Statistical_physics" title="Statistical physics">statistical physics</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_theory_of_gases" title="Kinetic theory of gases">kinetic theory of gases</a> applies Newton's laws of motion to large numbers (typically on the order of <a href="/wiki/Avogadro_constant" title="Avogadro constant">Avogadro's number</a>) of particles. Kinetic theory can explain, for example, the <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> that a gas exerts upon the container holding it as the aggregate of many impacts of atoms, each imparting a tiny amount of momentum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-:5-68">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 62">: 62 </span></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Langevin_equation" title="Langevin equation">Langevin equation</a> is a special case of Newton's second law, adapted for the case of describing a small object bombarded stochastically by even smaller ones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-75">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 235">: 235 </span></sup> It can be written<div class="mwe-math-element"><div class="mwe-math-mathml-display mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m{\vec {a}}=-\gamma {\vec {v}}+{\vec {\xi }}\,,}" display="block" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>m</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>a</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>

<mover>

</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>

<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</semantics>
</math></div><img alt="{\displaystyle m{\vec {a}}=-\gamma {\vec {v}}+{\vec {\xi }}\,,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-display" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/adf01d58105f6c4a735fd1eef8c3197a8c7ded03" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:15.848ex; height:3.509ex;"/></div>where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle \gamma }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

This is used to model <a href="/wiki/Brownian_motion" title="Brownian motion">Brownian motion</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electromagnetism">Electromagnetism</span></h3>
<p>Newton's three laws can be applied to phenomena involving <a href="/wiki/Electricity" title="Electricity">electricity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Magnetism" title="Magnetism">magnetism</a>, though subtleties and caveats exist.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_law" title="Coulomb's law">Coulomb's law</a> for the electric force between two stationary, <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">electrically charged</a> bodies has much the same mathematical form as Newton's law of universal gravitation: the force is proportional to the product of the charges, inversely proportional to the square of the distance between them, and directed along the straight line between them. The Coulomb force that a charge <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{1}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>
<mi>q</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="q_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9daa41f6e8f78ea6bb5711d7ac97901ce564b94e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.091ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span> exerts upon a charge <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q_{2}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<msub>

<mn>1</mn>
</mrow>
</msub>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle q_{1}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="q_{1}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/9daa41f6e8f78ea6bb5711d7ac97901ce564b94e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:2.091ex; height:2.009ex;"/></span>, and it points in the exact opposite direction. Coulomb's law is thus consistent with Newton's third law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Electromagnetism treats forces as produced by <i>fields</i> acting upon charges. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lorentz_force_law" title="Lorentz force law">Lorentz force law</a> provides an expression for the force upon a charged body that can be plugged into Newton's second law in order to calculate its acceleration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 85">: 85 </span></sup> According to the Lorentz force law, a charged body in an electric field experiences a force in the direction of that field, a force proportional to its charge <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle q}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>

</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cyclotron_motion.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2632" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/220px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/330px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/Cyclotron_motion.jpg/440px-Cyclotron_motion.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cyclotron_motion.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Lorentz force law in effect: electrons are bent into a circular trajectory by a magnetic field.</div></div></div><p>If the electric field vanishes (<span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {E}}=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<mo>=</mo>
<mi>q</mi>
<mi>B</mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

</mrow>
<mi>m</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle \omega =qB/m}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle \omega =qB/m}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b49dfeea500cc3c3fe955713b88bc3354233e7e6" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:10.581ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-75">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 222">: 222 </span></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mass_spectrometry" title="Mass spectrometry">Mass spectrometry</a> works by applying electric and/or magnetic fields to moving charges and measuring the resulting acceleration, which by the Lorentz force law yields the <a href="/wiki/Mass-to-charge_ratio" title="Mass-to-charge ratio">mass-to-charge ratio</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Collections of charged bodies do not always obey Newton's third law: there can be a change of one body's momentum without a compensatory change in the momentum of another. The discrepancy is accounted for by momentum carried by the electromagnetic field itself. The momentum per unit volume of the electromagnetic field is proportional to the <a href="/wiki/Poynting_vector" title="Poynting vector">Poynting vector</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panofsky1962_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Panofsky1962-80">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 184">: 184 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is subtle conceptual conflict between electromagnetism and Newton's first law: <a href="/wiki/Maxwell%27s_equations" title="Maxwell's equations">Maxwell's theory of electromagnetism</a> predicts that electromagnetic waves will travel through empty space at a constant, definite speed. Thus, some inertial observers seemingly have a privileged status over the others, namely those who measure the <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">speed of light</a> and find it to be the value predicted by the Maxwell equations. In other words, light provides an absolute standard for speed, yet the principle of inertia holds that there should be no such standard. This tension is resolved in the theory of special relativity, which revises the notions of <i>space</i> and <i>time</i> in such a way that all inertial observers will agree upon the speed of light in vacuum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[note 13]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Special_relativity">Special relativity</span></h3>
<p>In special relativity, the rule that Wilczek called "Newton's Zeroth Law" breaks down: the mass of a composite object is not merely the sum of the masses of the individual pieces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-85">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 33">: 33 </span></sup> Newton's first law, inertial motion, remains true. A form of Newton's second law, that force is the rate of change of momentum, also holds, as does the conservation of momentum. However, the definition of momentum is modified. Among the consequences of this is the fact that the more quickly a body moves, the harder it is to accelerate, and so, no matter how much force is applied, a body cannot be accelerated to the speed of light. Depending on the problem at hand, momentum in special relativity can be represented as a three-dimensional vector, <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle {\vec {p}}=m\gamma {\vec {v}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>p</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mi>γ<!-- γ --></mi>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mover>
<mi>v</mi>
<mo stretchy="false">→<!-- → --></mo>
</mover>
</mrow>
</mrow>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle {\vec {p}}=m\gamma {\vec {v}}}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="{\displaystyle {\vec {p}}=m\gamma {\vec {v}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f27b8359440200f473ce6fb30f54408f53b300e9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; margin-left: -0.089ex; width:8.991ex; height:2.843ex;"/></span>, where <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle m}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

</mrow>

</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a223c880b0ce3da8f64ee33c4f0010beee400b1a" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:1.262ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> is the <a href="/wiki/Lorentz_factor" title="Lorentz factor">Lorentz factor</a>, which depends upon the body's speed. Alternatively, momentum and force can be represented as <a href="/wiki/Four-vector" title="Four-vector">four-vectors</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 107">: 107 </span></sup>
</p><p>Newtonian mechanics is a good approximation to special relativity when the speeds involved are small compared to that of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 131">: 131 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="General_relativity">General relativity</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">General relativity</a> is theory of gravity that advances beyond that of Newton. In general relativity, gravitational force is reimagined as curvature of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">spacetime</a>. A curved path like an orbit is not the result of a force deflecting a body from an ideal straight-line path, but rather the body's attempt to fall freely through a background that is itself curved by the presence of other masses. A remark by <a href="/wiki/John_Archibald_Wheeler" title="John Archibald Wheeler">John Archibald Wheeler</a> that has become proverbial among physicists summarizes the theory: "Spacetime tells matter how to move; matter tells spacetime how to curve."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-88">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[76]</a></sup> Wheeler himself thought of this reciprocal relationship as a modern, generalized form of Newton's third law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheeler_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheeler-88">[75]</a></sup> The relation between matter distribution and spacetime curvature is given by the <a href="/wiki/Einstein_field_equations" title="Einstein field equations">Einstein field equations</a>, which require <a href="/wiki/Tensor_calculus" title="Tensor calculus">tensor calculus</a> to express.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-85">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 43">: 43 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Newtonian theory of gravity is a good approximation to the predictions of general relativity when gravitational effects are weak and objects are moving slowly compared to the speed of light.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-82">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 327">: 327 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quantum_mechanics">Quantum mechanics</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Quantum_mechanics" title="Quantum mechanics">Quantum mechanics</a> is a theory of physics originally developed in order to understand microscopic phenomena: behavior at the scale of molecules, atoms or subatomic particles. Generally and loosely speaking, the smaller a system is, the more an adequate mathematical model will require understanding quantum effects. The conceptual underpinning of quantum physics is <a href="/wiki/Bell%27s_theorem" title="Bell's theorem">very different from that of classical physics</a>. Instead of thinking about quantities like position, momentum, and energy as properties that an object <i>has</i>, one considers what result might <i>appear</i> when a <a href="/wiki/Measurement_in_quantum_mechanics" title="Measurement in quantum mechanics">measurement</a> of a chosen type is performed. Quantum mechanics allows the physicist to calculate the probability that a chosen measurement will elicit a particular result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[80]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Expectation_value_(quantum_mechanics)" title="Expectation value (quantum mechanics)">expectation value</a> for a measurement is the average of the possible results it might yield, weighted by their probabilities of occurrence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Ehrenfest_theorem" title="Ehrenfest theorem">Ehrenfest theorem</a> provides a connection between quantum expectation values and Newton's second law, a connection that is necessarily inexact, as quantum physics is fundamentally different from classical. In quantum physics, position and momentum are represented by mathematical entities known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hermitian_operator" title="Hermitian operator">Hermitian operators</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Born_rule" title="Born rule">Born rule</a> is used to calculate the expectation values of a position measurement or a momentum measurement. These expectation values will generally change over time; that is, depending on the time at which (for example) a position measurement is performed, the probabilities for its different possible outcomes will vary. The Ehrenfest theorem says, roughly speaking, that the equations describing how these expectation values change over time have a form reminiscent of Newton's second law. However, the more pronounced quantum effects are in a given situation, the more difficult it is to derive meaningful conclusions from this resemblance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[note 14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>

</p>
</div>
</div></li>
</ul></div></div></div>
<p>The concepts invoked in Newton's laws of motion — mass, velocity, momentum, force — have predecessors in earlier work, and the content of Newtonian physics was further developed after Newton's time. Newton combined knowledge of celestial motions with the study of events on Earth and showed that one theory of mechanics could encompass both.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[note 15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Antiquity_and_medieval_background">Antiquity and medieval background</span></h3>
<p>The subject of physics is often traced back to <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>; however, the history of the concepts involved is obscured by multiple factors. An exact correspondence between Aristotelian and modern concepts is not simple to establish: Aristotle did not clearly distinguish what we would call speed and force, and he used the same term for <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">density</a> and <a href="/wiki/Viscosity" title="Viscosity">viscosity</a>; he conceived of motion as always through a medium, rather than through space. In addition, some concepts often termed "Aristotelian" might better be attributed to his followers and commentators upon him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[86]</a></sup> These commentators found that Aristotelian physics had difficulty explaining projectile motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[note 16]</a></sup> Aristotle divided motion into two types: "natural" and "violent". The "natural" motion of terrestrial solid matter was to fall downwards, whereas a "violent" motion could push a body sideways. Moreover, in Aristotelian physics, a "violent" motion requires an immediate cause; separated from the cause of its "violent" motion, a body would revert to its "natural" behavior. Yet a javelin continues moving after it leaves the hand of its thrower. Aristotle concluded that the air around the javelin must be imparted with the ability to move the javelin forward. <a href="/wiki/John_Philoponus" title="John Philoponus">John Philoponus</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Greek" title="Byzantine Greek">Byzantine Greek</a> thinker active during the sixth century, found this absurd: the same medium, air, was somehow responsible both for sustaining motion and for impeding it. If Aristotle's idea were true, Philoponus said, armies would launch weapons by blowing upon them with bellows. Philoponus argued that setting a body into motion imparted a quality, <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_impetus" title="Theory of impetus">impetus</a>, that would be contained within the body itself. As long as its impetus was sustained, the body would continue to move.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 47">: 47 </span></sup> In the following centuries, versions of impetus theory were advanced by individuals including <a href="/wiki/Nur_ad-Din_al-Bitruji" title="Nur ad-Din al-Bitruji">Nur ad-Din al-Bitruji</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Abu%27l-Barak%C4%81t_al-Baghd%C4%81d%C4%AB" title="Abu'l-Barakāt al-Baghdādī">Abu'l-Barakāt al-Baghdādī</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Buridan" title="John Buridan">John Buridan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Albert_of_Saxony_(philosopher)" title="Albert of Saxony (philosopher)">Albert of Saxony</a>. In retrospect, the idea of impetus can be seen as a forerunner of the modern concept of momentum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[note 17]</a></sup> (The intuition that objects move according to some kind of impetus persists in many students of introductory physics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[90]</a></sup>)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inertia_and_the_first_law">Inertia and the first law</span></h3>
<p>The modern concept of inertia is credited to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galileo" title="Galileo">Galileo</a>. Based on his experiments, Galileo concluded that the "natural" behavior of a moving body was to keep moving, until something else interfered with it. Galileo recognized that in projectile motion, the Earth's gravity affects vertical but not horizontal motion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[91]</a></sup> However, Galileo's idea of inertia was not exactly the one that would be codified into Newton's first law. Galileo thought that a body moving a long distance inertially would follow the curve of the Earth. This idea was corrected by <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Beeckman" title="Isaac Beeckman">Isaac Beeckman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="René Descartes">René Descartes</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Gassendi" title="Pierre Gassendi">Pierre Gassendi</a>, who recognized that inertial motion should be motion in a straight line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Force_and_the_second_law">Force and the second law</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>, in his <i><a href="/wiki/Horologium_Oscillatorium" title="Horologium Oscillatorium">Horologium Oscillatorium</a></i> (1673), put forth the hypothesis that "By the action of gravity, whatever its sources, it happens that bodies are moved by a motion composed both of a uniform motion in one direction or another and of a motion downward due to gravity." Newton's second law generalized this hypothesis from gravity to all forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>One important characteristic of Newtonian physics is that forces can <a href="/wiki/Action_at_a_distance" title="Action at a distance">act at a distance</a> without requiring physical contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[note 18]</a></sup> For example, the Sun and the Earth pull on each other gravitationally, despite being separated by millions of kilometres. This contrasts with the idea, championed by Descartes among others, that the Sun's gravity held planets in orbit by swirling them in a vortex of transparent matter, <i><a href="/wiki/Aether_theories" title="Aether theories">aether</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[100]</a></sup> Newton considered aetherial explanations of force but ultimately rejected them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman2016_115-1"><a href="#cite_note-Newman2016-115">[98]</a></sup> The study of magnetism by <a href="/wiki/William_Gilbert_(physician)" title="William Gilbert (physician)">William Gilbert</a> and others created a precedent for thinking of <i>immaterial</i> forces,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman2016_115-2"><a href="#cite_note-Newman2016-115">[98]</a></sup> and unable to find a quantitatively satisfactory explanation of his law of gravity in terms of an aetherial model, Newton eventually declared, "<a href="/wiki/Hypotheses_non_fingo" title="Hypotheses non fingo">I feign no hypotheses</a>": whether or not a model like Descartes's vortices could be found to underlie the <i>Principia</i>'s theories of motion and gravity, the first grounds for judging them must be the successful predictions they made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[101]</a></sup> And indeed, since Newton's time <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_explanations_of_gravitation" title="Mechanical explanations of gravitation">every attempt at such a model has failed</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Momentum_conservation_and_the_third_law">Momentum conservation and the third law</span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> suggested that gravitational attractions were reciprocal — that, for example, the Moon pulls on the Earth while the Earth pulls on the Moon — but he did not argue that such pairs are equal and opposite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[102]</a></sup> In his <i><a href="/wiki/Principles_of_Philosophy" title="Principles of Philosophy">Principles of Philosophy</a></i> (1644), Descartes introduced the idea that during a collision between bodies, a "quantity of motion" remains unchanged. Descartes defined this quantity somewhat imprecisely by adding up the products of the speed and "size" of each body, where "size" for him incorporated both volume and surface area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[103]</a></sup> Moreover, Descartes thought of the universe as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plenum_(physics)" title="Plenum (physics)">plenum</a>, that is, filled with matter, so all motion required a body to displace a medium as it moved. During the 1650s, Huygens studied collisions between hard spheres and deduced a principle that is now identified as the conservation of momentum.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[105]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a> would later deduce the same rules for <a href="/wiki/Elastic_collision" title="Elastic collision">elastic collisions</a> that Huygens had, and <a href="/wiki/John_Wallis" title="John Wallis">John Wallis</a> would apply momentum conservation to study <a href="/wiki/Inelastic_collision" title="Inelastic collision">inelastic collisions</a>. Newton cited the work of Huygens, Wren, and Wallis to support the validity of his third law.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Newton arrived at his set of three laws incrementally. In a <a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">1684 manuscript written to Huygens</a>, he listed four laws: the principle of inertia, the change of motion by force, a statement about relative motion that would today be called <a href="/wiki/Galilean_invariance" title="Galilean invariance">Galilean invariance</a>, and the rule that interactions between bodies do not change the motion of their center of mass. In a later manuscript, Newton added a law of action and reaction, while saying that this law and the law regarding the center of mass implied one another. Newton probably settled on the presentation in the <i>Principia,</i> with three primary laws and then other statements reduced to corollaries, during 1685.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[107]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="After_the_Principia">After the <i>Principia</i></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven,_Mary_Somerville_1831.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="331" decoding="async" height="367" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven%2C_Mary_Somerville_1831.png/220px-Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven%2C_Mary_Somerville_1831.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/0/08/Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven%2C_Mary_Somerville_1831.png/330px-Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven%2C_Mary_Somerville_1831.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/0/08/Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven%2C_Mary_Somerville_1831.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Page_157_from_Mechanism_of_the_Heaven,_Mary_Somerville_1831.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Page 157 from <i>Mechanism of the Heavens</i> (1831), <a href="/wiki/Mary_Somerville" title="Mary Somerville">Mary Somerville</a>'s expanded version of the first two volumes of Laplace's <i>Traité de mécanique céleste.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[108]</a></sup> Here, Somerville deduces the inverse-square law of gravity from <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Newton expressed his second law by saying that the force on a body is proportional to its change of motion, or momentum. By the time he wrote the <i>Principia,</i> he had already developed calculus (which he called "<a href="/wiki/Fluxion" title="Fluxion">the science of fluxions</a>"), but in the <i>Principia</i> he made no explicit use of it, perhaps because he believed geometrical arguments in the tradition of <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a> to be more rigorous.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 15">: 15 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[110]</a></sup> Consequently, the <i>Principia</i> does not express acceleration as the second derivative of position, and so it does not give the second law as <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\displaystyle F=ma}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
<semantics>
<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
<mi>F</mi>
<mo>=</mo>
<mi>m</mi>
<mi>a</mi>
</mstyle>
</mrow>
<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\displaystyle F=ma}</annotation>
</semantics>
</math></span><img alt="F=ma" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1ca4e42b7d6d66f52294364928cb5f7c590f514c" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:8.109ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span>. This form of the second law was written (for the special case of constant force) at least as early as 1716, by <a href="/wiki/Jakob_Hermann" title="Jakob Hermann">Jakob Hermann</a>; <a href="/wiki/Leonhard_Euler" title="Leonhard Euler">Leonhard Euler</a> would employ it as a basic premise in the 1740s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[111]</a></sup> Euler pioneered the study of rigid bodies<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[112]</a></sup> and established the basic theory of fluid dynamics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[113]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>'s five-volume <i><a href="/wiki/Trait%C3%A9_de_m%C3%A9canique_c%C3%A9leste" title="Traité de mécanique céleste">Traité de mécanique céleste</a></i> (1798–1825) forsook geometry and developed mechanics purely through algebraic expressions, while resolving questions that the <i>Principia</i> had left open, like a full theory of the <a href="/wiki/Tide" title="Tide">tides</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>The concept of energy became a key part of Newtonian mechanics in the post-Newton period. Huygens' solution of the collision of hard spheres showed that in that case, not only is momentum conserved, but kinetic energy is as well (or, rather, a quantity that in retrospect we can identify as one-half the total kinetic energy). The question of what is conserved during all other processes, like inelastic collisions and motion slowed by friction, was not resolved until the 19th century. Debates on this topic overlapped with philosophical disputes between the metaphysical views of Newton and Leibniz, and variants of the term "force" were sometimes used to denote what we would call types of energy. For example, in 1742, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89milie_du_Ch%C3%A2telet" title="Émilie du Châtelet">Émilie du Châtelet</a> wrote, "Dead force consists of a simple tendency to motion: such is that of a spring ready to relax; <a href="/wiki/Vis_viva" title="Vis viva">living force</a> is that which a body has when it is in actual motion." In modern terminology, "dead force" and "living force" correspond to potential energy and kinetic energy respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[115]</a></sup> Conservation of energy was not established as a universal principle until it was understood that the energy of mechanical work can be dissipated into heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[117]</a></sup> With the concept of energy given a solid grounding, Newton's laws could then be derived within formulations of classical mechanics that put energy first, as in the Lagrangian and Hamiltonian formulations described above.
</p><p>Modern presentations of Newton's laws use the mathematics of vectors, a topic that was not developed until the late 19th and early 20th centuries. Vector algebra, pioneered by <a href="/wiki/Josiah_Willard_Gibbs" title="Josiah Willard Gibbs">Josiah Willard Gibbs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oliver_Heaviside" title="Oliver Heaviside">Oliver Heaviside</a>, stemmed from and largely supplanted the earlier system of <a href="/wiki/Quaternion" title="Quaternion">quaternions</a> invented by <a href="/wiki/William_Rowan_Hamilton" title="William Rowan Hamilton">William Rowan Hamilton</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[119]</a></sup>
</p>

Obviously the validity of this statement depends on the context..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-15"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-15">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The study of mechanics is complicated by the fact that household words like <i>energy</i> are used with a technical meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[10]</a></sup> Moreover, words which are synonymous in everyday speech are not so in physics: <i>force</i> is not the same as <i>power</i> or <i>pressure</i>, for example, and <i>mass</i> has a different meaning than <i>weight</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-openstax_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-openstax-14">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 150">: 150 </span></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-23">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">One textbook observes that a block sliding down an inclined plane is what "some cynics view as the dullest problem in all of physics".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 70">: 70 </span></sup> Another quips, "Nobody will ever know how many minds, eager to learn the secrets of the universe, found themselves studying inclined planes and pulleys instead, and decided to switch to some more interesting profession."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 173">: 173 </span></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See, for example, the discussion in José and Saletan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-18">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 9">: 9 </span></sup> Frautschi et al.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 134">: 134 </span></sup> as well as Feynman, Leighton and Sands,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FLS_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FLS-24">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Location: 12-1">: 12-1 </span></sup> argue that the second law is incomplete without a specification of a force by another law, like the law of gravity. Kleppner and Kolenkow argue that the second law is incomplete without the third law: an observer who sees one body accelerate without a matching acceleration of some other body to compensate would conclude, not that a force is acting, but that they are not an inertial observer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-22">[18]</a></sup> Landau and Lifshitz bypass the question by starting with the Lagrangian formalism rather than the Newtonian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Landau_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Landau-25">[20]</a></sup></span>

<li id="cite_note-50"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-50">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Among the many textbook treatments of this point are Hand and Finch<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hand-finch_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-hand-finch-43">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 81">: 81 </span></sup> and also Kleppner and Kolenkow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kleppner_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kleppner-22">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 103">: 103 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-84"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-84">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Discussions can be found in, for example, Frautschi et al.,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_16-9"><a href="#cite_note-:0-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 215">: 215 </span></sup> Panofsky and Phillips,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Panofsky1962_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Panofsky1962-80">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 272">: 272 </span></sup> Goldstein, Poole and Safko,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-82">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page: 277">: 277 </span></sup> and Werner.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[71]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-97"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-97">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Details can be found in the textbooks by, e.g., Cohen-Tannoudji et al.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cohen-Tannoudji_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-Tannoudji-95">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 242">: 242 </span></sup> and Peres.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 302">: 302 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-100"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-100">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As one physicist writes, "Physical theory is possible because we <i>are</i> immersed and included in the whole process – because we can act on objects around us. Our ability to intervene in nature clarifies even the motion of the planets around the sun – masses so great and distances so vast that our roles as participants seem insignificant. Newton was able to transform Kepler's kinematical description of the solar system into a far more powerful dynamical theory because he added concepts from Galileo's experimental methods – force, mass, momentum, and gravitation. The truly external observer will only get as far as Kepler. Dynamical concepts are formulated on the basis of what we can set up, control, and measure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[84]</a></sup> See, for example, Caspar and Hellman.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[85]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-103">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Aristotelian physics also had difficulty explaining buoyancy, a point that Galileo tried to resolve without complete success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[87]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-106"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-106">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Anneliese_Maier" title="Anneliese Maier">Anneliese Maier</a> cautions, "Impetus is neither a force, nor a form of energy, nor momentum in the modern sense; it shares something with all these other
concepts, but it is identical with none of them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 79">: 79 </span></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-117"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-117">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Newton himself was an enthusiastic <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemist</a>. <a href="/wiki/John_Maynard_Keynes" title="John Maynard Keynes">John Maynard Keynes</a> called him "the last of the magicians" to describe his place in the transition between <a href="/wiki/Protoscience" title="Protoscience">protoscience</a> and modern science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[95]</a></sup> The suggestion has been made that alchemy inspired Newton's notion of "action at a distance", i.e., one body exerting a force upon another without being in direct contact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[96]</a></sup> This suggestion enjoyed considerable support among historians of science<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[97]</a></sup> until a more extensive study of Newton's papers became possible, after which it fell out of favor. However, it does appear that Newton's alchemy influenced his <a href="/wiki/Optics" title="Optics">optics</a>, in particular, how he thought about the combination of colors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newman2016_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newman2016-115">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[99]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-Landau-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Landau_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Landau_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFLandauLifshitz1969"><a href="/wiki/Lev_Landau" title="Lev Landau">Landau, Lev D.</a>; <a href="/wiki/Evgeny_Lifshitz" title="Evgeny Lifshitz">Lifshitz, Evgeny M.</a> (1969). <i>Mechanics</i>. <a href="/wiki/Course_of_Theoretical_Physics" title="Course of Theoretical Physics">Course of Theoretical Physics</a>. Vol. 1. Translated by Sykes, J. B.; <a href="/wiki/John_Stewart_Bell" title="John Stewart Bell">Bell, J. S.</a> (2nd ed.). <a href="/wiki/Pergamon_Press" title="Pergamon Press">Pergamon Press</a>. p. vii. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-080-06466-6" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-080-06466-6"><bdi>978-0-080-06466-6</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/898931862" rel="nofollow">898931862</a>. <q>Only with this approach, indeed, can the exposition form a logical whole and avoid tautological definitions of the fundamental mechanical quantities. It is, moreover, essentially simpler, and leads to the most complete and direct means of solving problems in mechanics.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Mechanics&amp;rft.series=Course+of+Theoretical+Physics&amp;rft.pages=vii&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Pergamon+Press&amp;rft.date=1969&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F898931862&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-080-06466-6&amp;rft.aulast=Landau&amp;rft.aufirst=Lev+D.&amp;rft.au=Lifshitz%2C+Evgeny+M.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-101"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-101">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFUgaglia2015">Ugaglia, Monica (2015). <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/43915795" rel="nofollow">"Aristotle's Hydrostatical Physics"</a>. <i>Annali della Scuola Normale Superiore di Pisa. Classe di Lettere e Filosofia</i>. <b>7</b> (1): 169–199. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0392-095X" rel="nofollow">0392-095X</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/43915795" rel="nofollow">43915795</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Annali+della+Scuola+Normale+Superiore+di+Pisa.+Classe+di+Lettere+e+Filosofia&amp;rft.atitle=Aristotle%27s+Hydrostatical+Physics&amp;rft.volume=7&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=169-199&amp;rft.date=2015&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F43915795%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.issn=0392-095X&amp;rft.aulast=Ugaglia&amp;rft.aufirst=Monica&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F43915795&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-102"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-102">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFStraulinoGambiRighini2011">Straulino, S.; Gambi, C. M. C.; Righini, A. (January 2011). <a class="external text" href="http://aapt.scitation.org/doi/10.1119/1.3492721" rel="nofollow">"Experiments on buoyancy and surface tension following Galileo Galilei"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/American_Journal_of_Physics" title="American Journal of Physics">American Journal of Physics</a></i>. <b>79</b> (1): 32–36. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2011AmJPh..79...32S" rel="nofollow">2011AmJPh..79...32S</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1119%2F1.3492721" rel="nofollow">10.1119/1.3492721</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hdl_(identifier)" title="Hdl (identifier)">hdl</a>:<a class="external text" href="//hdl.handle.net/2158%2F530056" rel="nofollow">2158/530056</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0002-9505" rel="nofollow">0002-9505</a>. <q>Aristotle in his <i>Physics</i> affirmed that solid water should have a greater weight than liquid water for the same volume. We know that this statement is incorrect because the density of ice is lower than that of water (hydrogen bonds create an open crystal structure in the solid phase), and for this reason ice can float. [...] The Aristotelian theory of buoyancy affirms that bodies in a fluid are supported by the resistance of the fluid to being divided by the penetrating object, just as a large piece of wood supports an axe striking it or honey supports a spoon. According to this theory, a boat should sink in shallow water more than in high seas, just as an axe can easily penetrate and even break a small piece of wood, but cannot penetrate a large piece.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=American+Journal+of+Physics&amp;rft.atitle=Experiments+on+buoyancy+and+surface+tension+following+Galileo+Galilei&amp;rft.volume=79&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.pages=32-36&amp;rft.date=2011-01&amp;rft_id=info%3Ahdl%2F2158%2F530056&amp;rft.issn=0002-9505&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1119%2F1.3492721&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2011AmJPh..79...32S&amp;rft.aulast=Straulino&amp;rft.aufirst=S.&amp;rft.au=Gambi%2C+C.+M.+C.&amp;rft.au=Righini%2C+A.&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Faapt.scitation.org%2Fdoi%2F10.1119%2F1.3492721&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-104"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-104">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFSorabji2010">Sorabji, Richard (2010). "John Philoponus". <i>Philoponus and the Rejection of Aristotelian Science</i> (2nd ed.). Online before print. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780199930418.013.35" rel="nofollow">10.1093/oxfordhb/9780199930418.013.35</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-199-93041-8" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-199-93041-8"><bdi>978-0-199-93041-8</bdi></a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/oclc/972369868" rel="nofollow">972369868</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Newton%27s+Laws+of+Motion&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Handbook+of+Newton&amp;rft.pages=Online+before+print&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2020-12&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F972369868&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780199930418.013.35&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-199-93041-8&amp;rft.aulast=Smith&amp;rft.aufirst=George+E.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-126"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-126">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFPatterson1969">Patterson, Elizabeth C. (December 1969). <a class="external text" href="https://www.cambridge.org/core/product/identifier/S0007087400010232/type/journal_article" rel="nofollow">"Mary Somerville"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/The_British_Journal_for_the_History_of_Science" title="The British Journal for the History of Science">The British Journal for the History of Science</a></i>. <b>4</b> (4): 311–339. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1017%2FS0007087400010232" rel="nofollow">10.1017/S0007087400010232</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISSN_(identifier)" title="ISSN (identifier)">ISSN</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.worldcat.org/issn/0007-0874" rel="nofollow">0007-0874</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:246612625" rel="nofollow">246612625</a>. <q>In no sense was it a mere translation of Laplace's work. Instead it endeavoured to explain his method ". . . by which these results were deduced from one general equation of the motion of matter" and to bring the reader's mathematical skill to the point where the exposition of Laplace's mathematics and ideas would be meaningful—then to give a digest in English of his great work. Diagrams were added when necessary to the original text and proofs of various problems in physical mechanics and astronomy included. ... [F]or almost a hundred years after its appearance the book continued to serve as a textbook for higher mathematics and astronomy in English schools.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+British+Journal+for+the+History+of+Science&amp;rft.atitle=Mary+Somerville&amp;rft.volume=4&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=311-339&amp;rft.date=1969-12&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A246612625%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft.issn=0007-0874&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1017%2FS0007087400010232&amp;rft.aulast=Patterson&amp;rft.aufirst=Elizabeth+C.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.cambridge.org%2Fcore%2Fproduct%2Fidentifier%2FS0007087400010232%2Ftype%2Fjournal_article&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>
</li>

<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFSmith2007">Smith, George (19 December 2007). <a class="external text" href="https://plato.stanford.edu/entries/newton/" rel="nofollow">"Isaac Newton"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Stanford_Encyclopedia_of_Philosophy" title="Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy">Stanford Encyclopedia of Philosophy</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">6 March</span> 2022</span>. <q>These advances in our understanding of planetary motion led Laplace to produce the four principal volumes of his <i>Traité de mécanique céleste</i> from 1799 to 1805, a work collecting in one place all the theoretical and empirical results of the research predicated on Newton's <i>Principia</i>. From that time forward, Newtonian science sprang from Laplace's work, not Newton's.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Stanford+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy&amp;rft.atitle=Isaac+Newton&amp;rft.date=2007-12-19&amp;rft.aulast=Smith&amp;rft.aufirst=George&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fplato.stanford.edu%2Fentries%2Fnewton%2F&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ANewton%27s+laws+of+motion"></span></span>
</li>
